It's full of fascinating insight about the mysteries of the mind, consciousness, and the inter-relationship of the physical and spiritual worlds.<br /><br />The book is based on Dr. Alexander's out of body experience (OBE) while he lay in a coma in the intensive care unit in the hospital where he worked as a neurosurgeon. He was suffering from a rare form of bacterial meningitis from which recovery is rare. Miraculously, after seven days with no apparent brain activity, he recovers with a jolt, just as his caregivers are preparing to withdraw life support.<br /><br />He returns with vivid memories of his experiences in a heavenly world characterized by a bright vivid landscape where he was guided by an angelic young woman and where he senses the higher powers of unconditional love. Unlike most other OBE experiences, he has no sense of his identity in the physical world and was not greeted by long lost family members. At least so he initially thinks. However, he later comes to understand that the angelic woman who guided him was a birth sister of whom he had not previously been aware.<br /><br />Before his life-altering experience Dr. Alexander was skeptical about such OBE accounts, considering them merely a function of the dying brain. After it he was a changed man eager to record the awe-inspiring world and the presence of God to which he had been exposed. He now recognizes that, far from being an unimportant by-product of physical processes (as he had previously believed), \\"consciousness is not only very real it's actually more real than the rest of physical existence and most likely the basis of it all.\\" His journey of understanding continues to the benefit of his readers.<br /><br />Barry Francis

0062080237|2.0|With American Gods, Neil Gaiman attempts a very cool premise: An ex-con by the name of Shadow stumbles on the fantastic and often dangerous subterranean world of Old World gods, which is facing the imminent threat of a new order of deities. The novel follows Shadow's Odyssey across America, as he's lead from one curious destination to another by his guide and boss, Wednesday, aka Odin of Norse mythology.<br /><br />Along the way, Gaiman sprinkles in a series of interludes, which flesh out this hidden divine history of America. These sections are far and away the strongest sections of the book; putting Gaiman's gifts as a story teller and knowledge of mythologies and macabre folklore on display.<br /><br />Unfortunately, the main story line is pretty thin. The aggravatingly nicknamed Shadow remains a relatively one dimensional character for the first three quarters of the story, as he is whisked from one inconsequential scene to another by narrative propulsion that relies almost solely on groan inducing coincidences and, somewhat ironically, heavy handed deus ex machina. It's not until the book's final act that Shadow becomes even remotely as interesting as some of the minor characters, and then it's against the backdrop of an astounding anticlimax; putting into question the entire reading experience to that point.<br /><br />It feels like Gaiman wants to say something profound about America, but is never able to articulate himself. The biggest problem is the mythologies the author chooses to focus on. The simple fact is Egyptian and Norse mythology play an extremely minute role in American cultural identity, regardless of the early Viking landings or folktales brought over by immigrants. Meanwhile, the role of Christianity in America or even its role in exterminating, say, Norse, Celtic, or Native American traditions is completely absent from a novel about gods in conflict. Anthropomorphizing modern manifestations of faith like Internet and television is a cool idea, but the fact that the focus of today's major religions are missing (save a brief, unhelpful mention of Jesus)guarantees that the story will remain benign if not downright juvenile; no matter how imaginative Gaiman's use of sex and violence is.<br /><br />Of course, Religion is one of THE hot button issue of our times and it's easy to imagine the ridiculous outrage that would follow a book where Jesus and Buddha were duking it out. At the same time, Gaiman can't even set up a coherent set of rules for his own imaginary universe. Why would a character who does not believe in the gods of Ancient Egypt experience the Egyptian Underworld? Why not the Greek Underworld? Exactly what is the deal with the various permutations of a single God running around the planet? Who in contemporary America is worshiping Odin enough to sustain his existence, anyway? Am I being too critical or this a rather slapdash universe?<br /><br />The biggest problem with American Gods is its posturing as dangerous and edgy when it has absolutely no teeth. It's a big book that doesn't particularly go anywhere, with a premise that never meets its potential. It's a light, innocuous read; the sort of forgettable fluff that's perfect for those long tedious stretches of a cross country trip., but the fact that the focus of today's major religions are missing (save a brief, unhelpful mention of Jesus)guarantees that the story will remain benign if not downright juvenile; no matter how imaginative Gaiman's use of sex and violence is.<br /><br />Of course, Religion is one of THE hot button issue of our times and it's easy to imagine the ridiculous outrage that would follow a book where Jesus and Buddha were duking it out. At the same time, Gaiman can't even set up a coherent set of rules for his own imaginary universe. Why would a character who does not believe in the gods of Ancient Egypt experience the Egyptian Underworld? Why not the Greek Underworld? Exactly what is the deal with the various permutations of a single God running around the planet? Who in contemporary America is worshiping Odin enough to sustain his existence, anyway? Am I being too critical or this a rather slapdash universe?<br /><br />The biggest problem with American Gods is its posturing as dangerous and edgy when it has absolutely no teeth. It's a big book that doesn't particularly go anywhere, with a premise that never meets its potential. It's a light, innocuous read; the sort of forgettable fluff that's perfect for those long tedious stretches of a cross country trip.

081297381X|4.0|In his book, The Black Swan: The Impact of the Highly Improbable, Dr. Nassim Nicholas Taleb illustrates the truly unpredictable nature of black swan events. He shows how the limits of human imagination and available information hide the possibility of these events from us regardless of the retrospective assertions made by `the experts.' Dr. Taleb goes on to reveal micro-level black swans that influence the day-to-day success and failure of companies and individual employees.<br /><br />The Black Swan brings to the forefront the reality of unpredictable events. Through his proof of their existence, Dr. Taleb creates a mandate for executives and managers to examine and prepare their organization to deal with black swan events at the corporate and employee level.<br /><br />The one shortcoming of Dr. Taleb's book is the absence of a set of guiding protocols or principles for effectively dealing with black swan events once they occur. This remains as an outstanding research and development project for the reader with no initial direction provided.<br /><br />I like The Black Swan for its exposure of unpredictable events and the inferred call to action to prepare one's organization to deal with these catastrophes commensurate with the organization's overall risk profile; making The Black Swan a StrategyDriven recommended read.<br /><br />All the Best,<br />Nathan Ives<br />StrategyDriven Principal

B003G4W49C|5.0|What can I say that hasn't already been said about this wonderful novel? I regret having not enjoyed it sooner, only discovering it (and the author) in my 30s. It's a timeless story that has aged well after three decades, and will soon be released as a film in 2013.<br /><br />What I like about the story is how the children learn to work together and progress slowly as the story unfolds, so the reader is made to share in their victories and losses. Also like that Card predicted children in future school would use an iPad-like tablet for their lessons. Not the first author to use the idea, but the first as far as I know to use a tablet entirely with touch/voice input. But that's ancillary...<br /><br />The strength of this novel is the character development. By the end, you really can empathize with what each individual child is going through, Ender, Bean, and the others. Highly recommend reading Ender's Shadow NEXT, rather than the so-called official sequel, Speaker for the Dead, which is a wholly different sci-fi story in another vein entirely. A good story, but disappointing if one is hoping for more Ender's Game. Shadow, on the other hand, is a direct parallel story from the viewpoint of Bean!<br /><br />Having read this about 10 years ago now, I intend to read it again on Kindle and go through the whole series again!

Scalzi doesn't worry too much about relativistic time dilation as does Haldeman, though still present it's not \\"Forever\\", i.e. thousands of years. B003H4I4JU|3.0|The point I'm making with my review title is that I felt Speaker for the Dead should have been held in reserve until Ender's Shadow and the other proper sequels had been written. Granted, those were 20+ years later, but the story was just so hard to relate to immediately after reading Ender's Game that my initial reaction was a negative one.<br /><br />This is a solid, grand sci-fi novel that stands entirely on its own without requiring Ender's Game as a lead-in, although the likelihood that someone will read this book first is slim, it likely has happened from time to time. Like I said, I did not enjoy this novel at first, because my expectations were not in line with the author's. I wanted more Ender's Game, more battle school, more stories about the children. Instead, the author sends our hero out into the far reaches of space to a colony planet, never to return to Earth. It's his story, I guess, but I have always considered that to be a very bad decision, as far as developing this beloved character.<br /><br />Secondly, I failed to appreciate this novel because it had religious overtones that I found disturbing. Not just an alien race with a social order/religion, no, what I mean is, Card's religious themes applied to Ender Wiggin, a boy who was raised without religion. In other words, raised without the imprint of fatalism or shame for human behaviors, natural behaviors that religion calls sin or evil. Shame, for instance, is an overwhelming trait of post-war Ender, when no such emotion should exist in a child raised Spartan-style to be a warrior from a young age onward.<br /><br />A child raised in that environment, taught to ignore pain, to defeat an enemy COMPLETELY, so that they can never again threaten you. That is what he did to the alien race that threatened Earth. He was a hero for saving humanity. There was no GUILT or REGRET involved in that action. Furthermore, to exacerbate the flaw in this character, he was not even AWARE that the battle was real, with real lives being lost, and a real alien race wiped out.<br /><br />No, these flaws and inconsistencies are absolutely unrealistic and borne from Card's own religious perspectives, transferred to his character, Ender. I do not pull out that card (forgive the pun) on my own, Card has made his religious perspectives public domain.<br /><br />Therefore, as a result of not understanding or believing the apparent GUILT and REGRET imprinted onto Ender, I had a hard time taking the rest of the story seriously because my suspension of disbelief was lost in the first chapter. The SoD was a greater loss to me than the religious characteristics applied to this beloved hero.<br /><br />In the end, Ender becomes a puppet caricature, wielded by the author, possessing the author's own insecurities, rather than possessing the true confidence of a warrior child --akin to a Spartan. That child would have felt glory after defeating a great threat, doing exactly what he was trained to do. Not guilt. Not regret. Overall, it was just really impossible for me to enjoy it as a result.<br /><br />Back to my premise, about the timing of the story. It may not have won the Hugo and Nebula awards 20 years later, but would have made a lot more sense after the Shadow series had played out, but that too was dependent upon the loss of Ender to space. Irrelevant, but just wishing, perhaps, for a parallel universe scenario in which Ender did not leave.with real lives being lost, and a real alien race wiped out.<br /><br />No, these flaws and inconsistencies are absolutely unrealistic and borne from Card's own religious perspectives, transferred to his character, Ender. B00B69UQEO|3.0|I've grown up with Depeche Mode - my mom was a New Wave chick who was in the clubs dancing along with Speak and Spell, while purchasing every DM album afterwards - and I've come to really love their music ever since I bought my first DM LP all on my own way back when (Ultra). So, this album has to impress me if it wants good marks for being a good addition to Depeche Mode's library, rather than it getting good marks just because all the other music acts in stores suck.<br /><br />With that being said, this album is merely okay. I'd hate to fall into the \\"Alan Wilder club,\\" but after he left there was definitely a downward progression of energy within the group. Ultra was a great break away from where SoFaD was taking the band because it was a reaction to Gahan's drug addition, how close DM was to breaking up entirely, and Alan's departure. So, when you compare that mindset to DM's last album, Sounds of the Universe, I gotta dare anyone who's never heard either album to say that they were written by the same people. And while I know that DM has moved away from their sexual innuendo beginnings, they've moved in to a category that I just don't care about - Gahan is more focused with his religious fanaticism and faith than ever, Gore isn't challenging himself and gives us single track minded song writing, and Fletcher's mixes are lazy sounding because the band prefers to let Gahan's voice lead the instrumentals rather than it being the other way around. The lyrics are fine for what they are, but the songs are so far and away from anything \\"Depeche Mode\\" that you gotta wonder if they really have anything to say anymore.<br /><br />There's little energy here. Outside of three tracks (The decent 'Angel', the almost excellent 'Should Be Higher', and the sleeper track 'Soothe My Soul') the album just sounds tired. And honestly, I'm tired. I'm tired of the reflectional music they've been making for a long while now, I'm tired of the slower/methodical tracks that have a deep meaning to the band alone, and I'm tired of not having loved a Depeche Mode album in 16 years. If you want my frame of mind, the tracks I still love and listen to today are 'Lie to Me', 'Nothing', 'Stories of Old', 'Useless', 'Sea of Sin'; and so I'm not looking for another Violator album full of commercial hits. It's just that I've found every album since Ultra has only been able to provide me with one, maybe two tracks, that I really enjoy while the rest are duds.<br /><br />Like I said earlier, this album is okay. But you'll not also be frustrated at all the ballad songs that Gahan needs to sing these days, but also because hidden behind his voice you can hear the original DM sound. It's buried beneath the boring flat noises that Gore and Fletcher use to enhance the singers lyrics, but it's there, and sometimes the best you can get is when it's tailing at the very end 10 seconds of a song on it's very own.
